Amm and Khass — General and Specific
beginner
6 min
Roadmap: 'Amm, Khas, Takhsis, and Naskh
Table of contents for usul al-fiqh topics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key topics in Hanafi usul al-fiqh related to general and specific texts and state the main point of the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on 'Amm.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school holds 'Amm as qat'i until a valid takhsis; Shafi'is view it as zanni inherently.

This passage is a table of contents from a commentary on usul al-fiqh, listing the major topics you will encounter: the nature of general and specific terms, how they interact, and the critical distinction between restriction (takhsis) and abrogation (naskh). As a Hanafi student, understanding the foundational dispute with the Shafi'is on the definitiveness of 'Amm is essential before diving into the details. This lesson will map the terrain so you know what to expect.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure
Identify that this is a table of contents. Note the sequence: first the dispute on 'Amm, then conflict between 'Amm and Khas, then takhsis methods, then the four schools' positions, finally distinction from naskh. This ordering reflects the logical progression in usul manuals.
The TOC is a study guide; internalize the order.
2
Locate the central dispute
The passage begins with the Hanafi-Shafi'i disagreement on whether 'Amm is qat'i (definitive) or zanni (conjectural). This is a pivotal usul issue that affects how texts are interpreted. As a Hanafi, you will learn that 'Amm is considered qat'i until a credible restrictor (mukhasis) appears.
This is the first major ikhtilaf in the lesson sequence.
3
Distinguish takhsis from naskh
The TOC explicitly marks a difference between takhsis (restriction of a general term to some individuals) and naskh (abrogation of an entire ruling). Note that takhsis does not cancel the general text; it narrows its application. Naskh replaces the ruling entirely.
Confusing these two is a common error; the text will provide fiqh examples to clarify.
4
Preview the fiqh examples
The TOC mentions examples from sale (buyūʿ) such as selling two slaves for a lump sum. These examples will illustrate how takhsis and naskh operate in concrete cases. The example of a slave and a free person is used to show that a separate price for each is not an analogy to naskh.
These examples are from Hanafi fiqh; pay attention to the reasoning.
5
Observe the plural restriction rule
The passage ends with a note that the plural can be restricted to a minimum of three, a specific Hanafi position. This is a detail in the section on expressions of generality (alfāẓ al-ʿumūm).
This rule is unique to the Hanafi school and differs from others.

Difference between Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the definitiveness (qat'i) and conjecturalness (zanni) of the general ('amm). Ruling on conflict between specific (khas) and general ('amm). Example of conflict with specific. Section: Restricting the general to some of what it encompasses. Methods of restricting the general to some individuals. Types of restrictor (mukhasis). In non-independent [restrictor] i.e., in what [they did not differentiate between its being] i.e., restriction. Statement of four schools regarding the ruling of the general restricted according to some. First school, second school, third school, fourth school is that of the Hanafis. Then he mentioned the result of the possibility of doubt in it by his statement: The general restricted according to some is a definitive proof. When the general restricted according to some is conjectural, why is deduction from it valid? So here the difference between restriction (takhsis) and abrogation (naskh) became apparent. Difference between restrictor and abrogator. And here are subsidiary issues suitable to what we mentioned. Example of exception (istithna). Evidence for impermissibility of sale. Example of abrogation: when he sold two slaves for one thousand. Statement of the fiqh issue in the example of abrogation. Example of restriction: when he sold two slaves for one thousand. Statement of the fiqh issue in the example of restriction. The sale of a slave and a free person, stating a separate price for each, is not similar to abrogation. Section on its expressions: they are either general in its wording. Statement of expressions of generality. So it is valid to restrict the plural. A posterity to his statement that the minimum of the plural is three. Restriction of the plural can be to three individuals, not less.

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents from the book 'Altarweehurdusharhaltaozeehwattalweeh', listing topics related to usul al-fiqh, specifically on the concepts of 'Amm (general), Khas (specific), Takhsis (restriction), and Naskh (abrogation). It begins by mentioning the disagreement between the Hanafis and Shafi'is on whether the general ('amm) is definitive (qat'i) or conjectural (zanni). It then moves to the ruling when a specific text conflicts with a general one, and provides examples. Next, it discusses methods of restricting a general term to some of its individuals, and the types of restrictors. The author mentions that the Hanafis have a specific position among four schools regarding the status of a general term that has been restricted according to some scholars. It then explains that despite the possibility of doubt, the restricted general can still be used as a proof. A key distinction is drawn between restriction (takhsis) and abrogation (naskh), with examples from fiqh, such as selling two slaves for a lump sum, and how the sale of a slave and a free person differs. The section concludes with a discussion of expressions of generality and the validity of restricting a plural to a minimum of three, which is a specific Hanafi ruling. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh, and the passage sets the stage for detailed discussions in the main text.

Key Terms
عام ('Amm): A term that encompasses all individuals of its category without restriction.
خاص (Khas): A term that refers to a specific individual or limited set.
تخصیص (Takhsis): The act of restricting a general term to some of its individuals.
نسخ (Naskh): Abrogation of a legal ruling by a later revealed text.
مخصص (Mukhasis): The evidence or factor that restricts the general term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Whether 'Amm is qat'i or zanni

D) The definition of ijma

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers the general text ('amm) to be definitive (qat'i) unless a valid restrictor (mukhasis) is proven, while the Shafi'i school views it as conjectural (zanni) by default.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Alif-lam in Definite Plurals: Three Levels of Meaning
Explaining the sequential meanings of alif-lam in definite plurals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three sequential meanings of alif-lam on a definite plural noun and identify which meaning applies based on context.

Core Idea
Alif-lam on a definite plural first signals covenant, then generalization, then genus — only generalization yields true 'amm.

The definite article 'al' attached to a plural noun is a key expression of generality ('amm) in Hanafi usul. But not every definite plural automatically means 'all'. In fact, the 'al' can indicate a specific known group (covenant), the entire category (generalization), or the abstract species (genus). This lesson unpacks the ordered hierarchy of these meanings and the conditions under which each applies.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the order of meanings
Commit to memory: covenant ('ahd) is the first level, then generalization (istighraq), then genus (jins). This order is essential for interpreting any definite plural.
The order is based on linguistic priority in Hanafi usul.
2
Understand the default when no context is given
If no specific group has been previously mentioned, covenant cannot apply; the meaning defaults to generalization. If generalization is impossible (e.g., the category has no individuals), then genus is intended.
Covenant requires a prior reference (known ma'had).
3
Recognize the metaphorical shift for genus
When the definite plural indicates genus, it is metaphorical (majaz). The sense of plurality (jam'iyyah) is somewhat broken, but may remain from one perspective (min wajh). This distinguishes it from generalization where plurality fully applies.
This nuance affects how we derive rulings from such texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهَا ( أَى مِنْ الْفَاظِ الْعَامِّ ) الف لام میں اول درجہ عہد کا ہے پھر استغراق کا پھر جنس کا. جمع معرف بالام کے عموم پر دلائل. جمع معرف بالام سے جنس مراد لینا مجاز ہے اور اس وقت اس کی جمعیت ٹوٹ جاتی ہے. جمع معرف بالسلام سے مراد جب جنس ہو تو من وجہ جمعیت رہتی ہے.
And among them (i.e., among the expressions of generality): The alif-lam first indicates covenant, then generalization, then genus. Evidences for generality of definite plural with alif-lam. Taking genus meaning from definite plural with alif-lam is metaphorical, and at that time its plurality is broken. When the definite plural with alif-lam intends genus, then in one respect plurality remains.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage discusses the usul rule regarding the alif-lam (definite article) attached to plural nouns. The author states that the alif-lam in such constructions has three levels of meaning in descending priority: first, it denotes a specific covenant (a previously mentioned or known group); second, it denotes generalization (istighraq, including all individuals); third, it denotes the genus (jins, the abstract category). The passage then notes that interpreting the definite plural as indicating genus is a metaphorical usage (majaz), and when it is interpreted as genus, the sense of plurality (jam'iyyah) is lost or broken. However, there is a nuance: if the genus is intended, the plurality may still remain from one perspective (min wajh). This is a key point in Hanafi usul for distinguishing between 'amm (general) and other meanings. The evidences (dalail) for the generality of the definite plural are also referenced but not detailed here.

Key Terms
الف لام (alif-lam): The definite article attached to nouns; in usul, it can indicate covenant, generalization, or genus.
عھد ('ahd): A known, previously specified individual or group (contractual reference).
استغراق (istighraq): Including all members of the category (generalization).
جنس (jins): The abstract category or species without specific individuals.
جمع معرف باللام (jam' mu'arraf bil-lam): A plural noun made definite with alif-lam; it is from the expressions of generality ('amm).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Genus (jins)
B) Generalization (istighraq)
C) Covenant ('ahd)
D) Metaphor (majaz)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the alif-lam on a definite plural has three ordered meanings: covenant, then generalization, then genus; only the generalization yields true 'amm.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Applying General Texts: The Usul Ruling on Cursing Yazid
A usul-based dispute on whether general prohibitions against cursing apply to a specific controversial figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify general (amm) textual prohibitions against cursing believers, apply the Hanafi principle that amm is binding until specified, and evaluate the argument that a specific individual may be cursed despite his sins.

Core Idea
General texts prohibiting cursing believers apply to every believer, even those who commit major sins.

This passage tackles a charged historical question—whether it is permissible to curse Yazid ibn Muawiyah—by applying fundamental usul al-fiqh principles about general and specific texts. The author cites several well-known hadiths that prohibit cursing believers as a general class, then argues that these general prohibitions bind all Muslims, including Yazid, unless a specific exclusion is proven. You will follow the reasoning that the Hanafi school uses to derive a ruling of impermissibility, and see how they refute counterarguments that rely on claims of kufr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general textual evidence
Survey the hadiths cited: 'A believer is not a curser', 'Do not curse with the curse of Allah', etc. Note that they all use general terms like 'believer' or 'something that does not deserve it'.
These fall under the category of 'nusoos ammah' (general texts).
2
Classify these texts as amm (general)
In Hanafi usul, these are classified as amm, meaning they apply to all individuals within the category unless specified otherwise.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle for amm
The principle is: al-amm yajibu an yujra 'ala 'umumihi hatta yarida dalil al-takhsis. No specific takhsis for Yazid has been established, so the general prohibition remains in force.
This is a qat'i (certain) rule in Hanafi usul.
4
Analyze the counterargument and the Hanafi response
The opponent argues from Yazid's alleged kufr. The Hanafi response distinguishes major sin from kufr and questions the historical evidence. This step shows the importance of takfir being based on clear proof.
Imam Ghazali's refutation is cited to undermine the opponent's historical claim.
5
State the derived usul ruling
Cursing a specific Muslim is impermissible (haram) based on these general texts, and this includes Yazid.
The ruling remains even if the individual is sinful, because major sin does not remove the general protection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس له من الامرشئ اويتوب عليهم او يعذبهم فانهم ظلمون فتيب عليهم كلهم رواه البخاری آ لعنت نہی کے نازل ہونے سے پہلے کی تو اللہ کی طرف سے نہی وارد ہوئی جیسا کہ حدیث سے واضح ہے اسی طرح ایک حدیث میں آپ نے ارشاد فرمایا کہ لا يكون المؤمن لعانا (رواه الترمذي وقال عليه السلام في مقام آخر لا تلعنوا بلعنة الله رواه ابوداؤ داسی طرح ایک اور حدیث میں ہے من لعن شيئا ليس له باهل رجعت اللعنة عليه (رواه التر بنی اسی طرح ایک اور مقام پر فرمایا لعن المؤمن گفتله رواه مسلم اسی طرح فرمایا که لا تسبوا الاموات فانهم قـدافــضــوا الـى مــاقــدمـوا ( رواه البخاری) ان تمام روایات سے معلوم ہوا کہ نصوص عامہ کی وجہ سے یزید پر لعنت جائز نہیں چنانچہ پہلے گروہ کا پہلی دو دلیلوں سے استدلال درست نہیں کیونکہ اس میں یزید پر با تعیین لعنت نہیں کی گئی یہ لعنت کی پہلی قسم میں شامل ہے اسی طرح تیسری دلیل سے بھی استدلال درست نہیں ایک تو اس وجہ سے کہ کسی مسلمان کو قتل کرنا یا قتل کرنے کا حکم دینا یہ گناہ کبیرہ ہے کفر نہیں دوسرا اس وجہ سے کہ اگر یوں کہا جائے کہ یزید حضرت حسین رضی اللہ عنہ کے قتل پر راضی تھا اور یہ حرام کو جائز سمجھنا ہے اور معصیت کو جائز سمجھنا کفر ہے تو امام غزالی اس کے جواب میں فرماتے ہیں کہ پہلے یہ بات ثابت نہیں کہ یزید نے حضرت حسین کو قتل کرنے کا حکم دیا تھا بلکہ یہ فعل شنیع امیر کوفہ عبید اللہ بن زیاد نے کیا اور یزید ایک مہینہ کی مسافت پر تھا اور اتنے تھوڑے سے وقت میں عبید اللہ کے پاس یزید کاقتل کرنے کا پیغام نہیں پہنچ سکتا بلکہ تاریخ سے ثابت ہے کہ یزید نے ابن زیاد کو کہا تھا رعت لی العداوة في قلب كل بروفاجر وقال رحمک اللہ یاحسین رضی الله عنه لقد قتلك رجل لم يعرف حق الرحم بہر حال یزید نے ابن زیاد کو اس پر سزا نہیں دی اس لئے اس کا یہ فعل شنیع قبیح ہے اور گناہ کبیرہ ہے اور یقیناً حضرت حسین حق پر تھے اور یزید ناحق تھا لیکن اصول اور قواعد شرعیہ کے مطابق اس پر لعنت جائز نہیں بعض لوگ جو اصول شرع سے ناواقف ہیں لعنت جائز نہ سمجھنے والوں پر خوارج کا فتوی لگاتے ہیں تو ہم اس کے بارہ میں وہی کہیں گے جو مولا نا عبد العزیز پر ھاروی علیہ الرحمہ نے فرمایا قبیح افعاله مشهور وحب اهل البيت واجب لكن النهي عن لعنه ليس للقصور في حبهم بل لقواعد الشرع کہ یزید پر لعنت سے منع کرنا یہ اہل بیت سے محبت کے کم ہونے کی وجہ سے نہیں بلکہ قواعد شرع کی وجہ سے ہے واللہ اعلم بالصواب۔
He has nothing of the matter, or He will turn to them or punish them, for they wronged, so He turned to all of them. Narrated by Bukhari. Before the verse of cursing was revealed, it did not come from Allah, as is clear from the hadith. Similarly, a hadith: the Prophet said, 'A believer is not a curser' (Tirmidhi). And he said in another place, 'Do not curse with the curse of Allah' (Abu Dawud). Similarly, another hadith: 'Whoever curses something that does not deserve it, the curse returns upon him' (Tirmidhi). Similarly, in another place he said, 'Cursing a believer is like killing him' (Muslim). Similarly, he said, 'Do not revile the dead, for they have reached what they have sent ahead' (Bukhari). From all these narrations, it is known that due to general texts, cursing Yazid is not permissible. Thus, the first group's argument from the first two proofs is incorrect because they do not specifically curse Yazid; it falls under the first type of cursing. Similarly, their argument from the third proof is also incorrect: first, because killing a Muslim or ordering his killing is a major sin, not kufr; second, even if one says that Yazid was pleased with the killing of Husayn (RA) and considered the forbidden as permissible, and considering a sin as permissible is kufr, Imam Ghazali responds that it is not established that Yazid ordered the killing of Husayn; rather, this heinous act was done by Ubaydullah ibn Ziyad, and Yazid was a month's journey away, so the message to kill could not have reached Ubaydullah in such a short time. Rather, history confirms that Yazid said to Ibn Ziyad, 'You have planted enmity in the heart of every righteous and wicked person' and said, 'May Allah have mercy on you, O Husayn, you were killed by a man who did not know the right of kinship.' In any case, Yazid did not punish Ibn Ziyad for this, so his act is heinous, ugly, and a major sin, and certainly Husayn was on the truth and Yazid was on falsehood; but according to principles and rules of Sharia, cursing him is not permissible. Some people who are unaware of the principles of Sharia label those who do not consider cursing permissible as Khawarij; we will say regarding this what Mawlana Abdul Aziz Parhawi (RA) said: 'His evil deeds are famous, and love of Ahl al-Bayt is obligatory, but prohibiting cursing him is not due to deficiency in love for them, but due to the rules of Sharia.' And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about the permissibility of cursing Yazid ibn Muawiyah. It first presents several hadiths that establish general prohibitions against cursing specific Muslims, then applies these general texts (nusoos ammah) to argue that cursing Yazid is impermissible. The author critiques the opposing group's proofs by distinguishing between general and specific cursing, and by arguing that major sin does not necessitate kufr. He refutes the claim that Yazid ordered the killing of Imam Husayn, citing historical distance and Ghazali's response. The conclusion is that despite Yazid's grave sins, the Sharia does not permit cursing him specifically, based on usul principles that prioritize general prohibitions and avoid takfir based on unproven premises. The passage ends with a quote from Abdul Aziz Parhawi affirming that this stance is consistent with love of Ahl al-Bayt and adherence to Sharia rules. No Hanafi ikhtilaf is mentioned within the passage; the discussion is presented as settled.

Key Terms
نصوص عامة (nusoos ammah): General textual evidence that applies broadly without particularizing a specific individual (e.g., hadiths prohibiting cursing believers in general).
لعن بعين (la'n bi 'ayn): Cursing a specific, named individual as opposed to a general category of sinners.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the hadith cited in the passage, what is the ruling on cursing a specific Muslim who has committed a major sin?
A) Permissible because he deserves it
B) Impermissible because of general prohibitions
C) Obligatory to protect the faith
D) Makruh but not haram

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul rule that general (amm) texts are binding on all instances prevents cursing a specific Muslim, even one with grave sins, unless clear proof of kufr is established.

Metaphor Analysis in Usul: Isti'arah, Tamthil, and Literal Interpretation
Detailed analysis of isti'ārah types in the author's description

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of isti'ārah (makkiyyah, takhyīliyyah, tarshīḥiyyah) used in legal metaphors and evaluate Allamah Khusraw's argument for preferring literal interpretation.

Core Idea
Not every vivid comparison is a metaphor; the literal reading may carry the intended legal nuance.

This passage examines the rhetorical analysis of the author's description of sharī'ah, al-Ḥanīfiyyah, and the kalimah as a tree and a building. The commentator classifies these comparisons into three types of isti'ārah, then presents tamthīl as an alternative, and finally Allamah Khusraw argues for a literal reading. Understanding these approaches is crucial for discerning when language in legal texts is figurative or literal, a key skill in uṣūl al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the preamble on ḥaqīqah and majāz
The commentator sets the foundation: words used in their original meaning are literal (ḥaqīqah); otherwise figurative (majāz). The relationship between them is either similarity (isti'ārah) or non-similarity (majāz mursal).
This classification is standard in Arabic rhetoric (balāghah) and applied to legal texts.
2
Identify the three isti'ārah types in the comparisons
For each comparison (sharī'ah/tree, al-Ḥanīfiyyah/tree, kalimah/building), determine whether the mushabbah or mushabbah bihi is mentioned, whether the similarity reason is hidden, and whether concomitants or attributes are affirmed. Assign makkiyyah, takhyīliyyah, and tarshīḥiyyah accordingly.
The commentator analyzes each part of the author's expression: uṣūl, furū', aḥkām, raf', asās, shāmikhat al-binā'.
3
Distinguish tamthīl from isti'ārah
Tamthīl likens one composite form from multiple things to another composite form. Here, the entire structure of sharī'ah (uṣūl + furū') is likened to a building (foundation + height). Contrast this with a simple one-to-one metaphor.
Some scholars consider the whole passage as tamthīl rather than individual isti'ārāt.
4
Evaluate Allamah Khusraw's literal position
Allamah Khusraw argues that the words are literal: sharī'ah truly has principles ('aqā'id) and branches (aḥkām), just as a tree has roots and branches. The uṣūl principle 'carry speech literally when possible' applies, especially when the literal meaning is comprehensive.
This position reflects a cautious approach to figurative interpretation in Hanafi usul.

پر مرتب ہے ان دو امروں میں سے ایک شرعیت ہے اور اس کے دو پہلو ہیں (۱) عقائد جو شرعیت کے لئے اصول کا درجہ رکھتے ہیں (۲) احکام عملیہ جو فروع کا درجہ رکھتے ہیں۔ دوسرا امر کلمہ
Metaphors: In this expression, the commentator (shāriḥ) has used metaphors (isti'ārāt). Before understanding them, it is necessary to know a preamble (muqaddimah). Preamble: If a word is used in its original meaning, it is ḥaqīqah; if not in its original meaning, it is majāz. Then between ḥaqīqah and majāz, there is either a relationship of similarity (tashbīh) or non-similarity. If the relationship is non-similarity, it is majāz mursal. If the relationship is similarity, it is isti'ārah. Then isti'ārah has four types: If the mushabbah bihi (the thing compared to) is mentioned and the mushabbah (the thing compared) is intended, it is isti'ārah taṣrīḥiyyah, e.g., "I saw a lion" (meaning a brave man). If the mushabbah is mentioned and the mushabbah is intended, but the reason for similarity (wajh al-tashbīh) is hidden in the mind, it is isti'ārah makkiyyah. If the necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of the mushabbah bihi are affirmed for the mushabbah, it is isti'ārah takhyīliyyah. If the appropriate attributes (munāsabāt) of the mushabbah bihi are affirmed for the mushabbah, it is isti'ārah tarshīḥiyyah. Now understand: The author compared the sharī'ah to a tree. The mushabbah is mentioned and the mushabbah is intended, but the reason for similarity is hidden—so it is isti'ārah makkiyyah. Among the necessary concomitants of the mushabbah bihi (tree) is that it has roots; thus in 'uṣūl' (roots) it is isti'ārah takhyīliyyah. Among the appropriate attributes of the tree is that it is strong; thus in 'ḥukm' (ruling) it is isti'ārah tarshīḥiyyah. Similarly, al-Ḥanīfiyyah (the Hanafi school) is compared to a tree, and the reason for similarity is not mentioned, so there is isti'ārah makkiyyah. The necessary concomitants of the tree, i.e., branches (furū'), are affirmed for the mushabbah (al-Ḥanīfiyyah), so that is isti'ārah takhyīliyyah, and its appropriate attributes, i.e., elevation (raf'), are affirmed for the mushabbah, so in 'raf'' it is isti'ārah tarshīḥiyyah. Kalimatuhu al-bāqiyah (the word of tawḥīd) is compared to a tall building. The mushabbah is mentioned and the mushabbah is intended, so there is isti'ārah makkiyyah. Among the necessary concomitants of the building is that it has a foundation (asās), so in 'asās' it is isti'ārah takhyīliyyah, and among its appropriate attributes is that it is high and lofty, so in 'shāmikhatu al-binā'' it is isti'ārah tarshīḥiyyah. And according to some, this entire expression is of the category of tamthīl. Definition of tamthīl: Tamthīl is to liken a form obtained from a combination of things to another form obtained from another combination of things. Here too, the form obtained from sharī'ah, its principles (uṣūl), and branches (furū') is likened to the form obtained from a building, its foundations, and its height. Investigation of 'Allāmah Khusraw: But 'Allāmah Khusraw says that here there is neither isti'ārah nor tamthīl, rather the words are taken in their literal (ḥaqīqah) sense. And the principle is that as long as possible, speech should be taken literally, especially when the literal meaning also includes subtle meanings. The summary is that we have two matters, each of which has two aspects, and each matter with both its aspects is subsidiary to the other matter and its two sides. One of these two matters is the sharī'ah, which has two aspects: (1) beliefs ('aqā'id), which are like principles (uṣūl) for the sharī'ah, and (2) practical rulings (aḥkām 'amaliyyah), which are like branches (furū'). The second matter is the kalimah...
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a meta-commentary analyzing the rhetorical devices (isti'ārāt) used by the author of the original matn. The commentator first provides a preamble on the distinction between ḥaqīqah (literal) and majāz (figurative), and then defines the types of isti'ārah based on the relationship of similarity. The author had described sharī'ah as a tree, the Hanafi school (al-Ḥanīfiyyah) as a tree, and the kalimah al-bāqiyah (word of tawḥīd) as a tall building. The commentator classifies each instance according to the four types of isti'ārah: makkiyyah (implicit metaphor), takhyīliyyah (imaginative metaphor affirming necessary concomitants), and tarshīḥiyyah (strengthening metaphor affirming appropriate attributes). Then it mentions that some scholars consider this entire expression as tamthīl (parable), defined as likening one composite form to another. Finally, 'Allāmah Khusraw (a Hanafi scholar) challenges both interpretations, arguing that the text is literal (ḥaqīqah) because the structure of sharī'ah truly consists of principles and branches, and the literal meaning includes subtle nuances. His position relies on the rule that words should be taken literally when possible, especially when the literal meaning is comprehensive. The commentator then summarizes that there are two matters: sharī'ah (with aspects of 'aqā'id as uṣūl and aḥkām as furū') and the kalimah (which is incomplete in the passage). This analysis shows the scholarly concern with precise legal language and avoiding unnecessary metaphorical interpretation when a literal reading suffices.

Key Terms
استعارہ مکیہ (isti'ārah makkiyyah): Metaphor where the mushabbah is mentioned, the mushabbah is intended, but the reason for similarity is hidden in the mind.
استعارہ تخیلیہ (isti'ārah takhyīliyyah): Metaphor where necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of the mushabbah bihi are affirmed for the mushabbah.
استعارہ ترشیجیہ (isti'ārah tarshīḥiyyah): Metaphor where appropriate attributes (munāsabāt) of the mushabbah bihi are affirmed for the mushabbah.
تمثیل (tamthīl): A parable likening one composite form from multiple elements to another composite form.
حقیقت (ḥaqīqah): Literal meaning; a word used in its original sense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason Allamah Khusraw rejects both isti'ārah and tamthīl interpretations of the author's description?
A) He believes the author was not skilled in rhetoric.
B) The literal meaning of the words already captures the intended structure.
C) The author explicitly said the words are literal.
D) The Hanafi school forbids using metaphors.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi uṣūl, a text is assumed literal until figurative interpretation is compelling, as per Allamah Khusraw's analysis.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Principle of Taḍmīn (Inclusion) in Determining Verb Transitivity
Analyzing composition, mubalaghah, and metaphors in explanatory context

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the concept of taḍmīn to explain how a typically monotransitive verb can take a second object in Qur'ānic interpretation.

Core Idea
Taḍmīn allows a verb to carry the meaning of another verb, thereby affecting its transitivity and expanding interpretive possibilities.

This lesson examines a specific syntactic problem from a classical text: how can the verb 'arsala', which normally takes one object, have a second object in the phrase 'muʿwānā ẓahīrā'? The solution lies in the principle of taḍmīn (inclusion) — interpreting the verb as carrying the meaning of 'jaʿala', which is ditransitive. This principle is crucial for understanding how Hanafi usul al-fiqh approaches textual ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syntactic problem
Understand that 'arsala' is normally transitive with one object, but the phrase 'muʿwānā ẓahīrā' is presented as a second object.
The verb arsala in classical Arabic typically takes one direct object.
2
Recognize the proposed solution
The commentator invokes taḍmīn, claiming 'arsala' includes the meaning of 'jaʿala'.
Taḍmīn is not literal substitution but merging meanings.
3
Verify the mechanism
Taḍmīn allows the verb to carry the transitivity pattern of the included verb, thus explaining the second object.
4
Apply to other texts
Consider how this principle can resolve similar syntactic anomalies in sacred texts, expanding interpretive options.
This principle is often used in Hanafi tafsir and usul.

جواب:۔ اس کو لفظ نصیر پر قیاس کیا گیا ہے جس طرح وہ صفت مشبہ ہے لیکن اس کے باوجو د متعدی ہے کیونکہ اس کا معنی ہے مدد کرنے والا ، اسی طرح یہ صفت مشبہ ہونے کے باوجود متعدی ہے لیکن یہ درست نہیں کیونکہ کو کہتے ہیں کہ جو قوم کا کلی طور پر معتمد علیہ ہو تو اس صورت میں ظہیر میں مبالغہ معوان سے زیادہ ہوگا۔ قوله من المهاجرين والانصار : يا اتم کا بیان ہے اس سے مراد سب صحابہ کرام ہیں لیکن زیادہ شرافت کیوجہ سے ذکر صرف مہاجرین اور انصار کا کیا۔ والذین اتبعو ہم اس کا عطف وانتم پر ہے اس سے مراد تا بعین ہیں مہاجرین والا نصار پر اس کا عطف نہیں ہوسکتا کیونکہ پیچھے تین چیزین ذکر ہوئیں (۱) اغتنام (۲) استصحاب (۳) استحسان یہ تین چیزیں صحابہ کرام کو حاصل ہیں باقیوں کو حاصل نہیں ۔ مرادی تحقیق : حتی صادفت حتی غایت کے لئے ہے اور صادفت نتیجہ ہے اوقد کا اور رایت الناس نتیجہ ہے اوضح کا، خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ جب علوم نظریہ اور علوم عملیہ کو حاصل کرنے کے لئے کامل النور چراغ (محمد ہے ) پایا گیا تو علوم اتنے کثیر ہو گئے کہ گویا ٹھاٹھیں مارتے سمندر ہیں اور جب لوگوں کو واضح راستہ مل گیا اور اس پر چلنے سے کسی قسم کی رکاوٹ نہ رہی تو لوگ فوج در فوج اس پر چلنے لگے۔ استعارات :- شاری نے علوم کو تشبیہ دی ہے دریا سے ذکر مشبہ کا اور مراد بھی مشبہ ہے تو علوم میں استعارہ مکیہ ہے اور دریا
Composition analysis: For 'muʿwānā ẓahīrā' there are two possibilities in its composition: first, it is a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) from the pronoun of 'arsala'; second, it is a second object for 'arsala'. Question: On the second probability, the objection arises that the verb 'arsala' is transitive with one object, so how can it take a second object? Answer: There is inclusion (taḍmīn) here, that 'arsala' is in the meaning of 'jaʿala', and 'jaʿala' is transitive with two objects, so on this basis it can be a second object. Question: 'muʿwānā' means 'much helper' and 'ẓahīr' means 'overpowering/dominant', so the hyperbole in 'muʿwānā' is greater than in 'ẓahīr'. Eloquence requires mentioning the word with lesser hyperbole first, then the one with greater hyperbole, so that there is progression from lower to higher, but here there is regression from higher to lower, which is not correct. Answer (1): 'ẓahīr' is the form of the descriptive adjective (ṣifah mushabbahah) and carries the meaning of permanence; its meaning is 'permanent helper', so it has greater hyperbole than 'muʿwānā', thus no problem. But on this, an objection arises: the descriptive adjective is intransitive, so its being transitive would be necessary. Answer: It is analogized to the word 'naṣīr'; just as that is a descriptive adjective but despite that it is transitive because its meaning is 'helper', similarly this is a descriptive adjective yet transitive. But this is not correct because according to the verifying scholars, 'naṣīr' is an active participle, not a descriptive adjective. Answer (2): It is true that 'ẓahīr' has lesser hyperbole, but the author delayed 'ẓahīr' observing the context (majmaʿ). The summary is that it is not from the category of progression, but from the category of completion (tamīm): first he mentioned one meaning with hyperbole, then he expressed the remaining meaning with the other word. Answer (3): It is also possible that it is derived from 'ẓahīr al-qawm', and 'ẓahīr al-qawm' refers to one who is completely relied upon by the people; in that case, the hyperbole in 'ẓahīr' is greater than in 'muʿwānā'. His saying 'from the Muhājirūn and Anṣār': it is an explanation of 'you all' (antum); its meaning is all the Companions, but due to greater honor, only the Muhājirūn and Anṣār were mentioned. 'And those who followed them' – this is a conjunction with 'antum'; its meaning is the Followers (Tābiʿūn). It cannot be conjoined to 'Muhājirūn and Anṣār' because three things were mentioned earlier: (1) ihtinām (taking seriously), (2) istiṣḥāb (continuity of obligation), (3) istiḥsān (juristic preference). These three things are possessed by the Companions, not by others. Interpretive analysis: 'ḥattā' in 'ḥattā ṣādafat' is for purpose/gāyah (limit), and 'ṣādafat' is the result of 'awqada', and 'raʾaytu al-nās' is the result of 'awḍaḥa'. The summary is: when the perfect light (i.e., Muḥammad) was found for acquiring theoretical and practical sciences, the sciences became so abundant that they were like roaring seas, and when people found a clear path with no obstacles, they flocked to it in crowds. Metaphors: The commentator compared the sciences to the sea; the mentioned object (mushabbah) and the intended meaning is also the mushabbah, so in 'sciences' there is a metaphorical loan (istiʿārah makkiyyah) and 'sea'...
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a multi-faceted linguistic and exegetical commentary typical of sharḥ works. It opens with a syntactic analysis of the phrase 'muʿwānā ẓahīrā' from a Qur'ānic or hadith context, discussing two possible syntactic roles: as a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) or as a second object. The author anticipates an objection regarding transitivity and answers it via the principle of taḍmīn (inclusion of meaning), wherein the verb 'arsala' is interpreted to carry the meaning of 'jaʿala' to accept a second object. The discussion then shifts to the comparative degree of emphasis (mubālaghah) between the two words, addressing a perceived violation of the principle that weaker emphasis should precede stronger emphasis (taraqqī). Three answers are provided: (1) by re-evaluating the form of 'ẓahīr' as a ṣifah mushabbahah implying permanence, (2) by reinterpreting the ordering as tamīm (completion) rather than taraqqī, and (3) by linking 'ẓahīr' to the phrase 'ẓahīr al-qawm' implying total reliance. Next, the passage explains the phrase 'from the Muhājirūn and Anṣār' as referring to all Companions, with a specific conjunction analysis of 'and those who followed them'. It clarifies that three principles (ihtinām, istiṣḥāb, istiḥsān) are unique to the Companions. Finally, a 'murādī taḥqīq' provides an interpretive summary of a poetic or narrative line involving 'ḥattā ṣādafat' and 'raʾaytu al-nās', concluding with an explanation of metaphors (istiʿārāt).

Key Terms
تضمین (taḍmīn): Including the meaning of one verb within another, thereby affecting its transitivity.
مفعول ثانی (maf'ūl thānī): Second object of a ditransitive verb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can 'arsala' take a second object in 'arsala muʿwānā ẓahīrā'?
A) Because 'arsala' is inherently ditransitive
B) Because it is in the meaning of 'jaʿala' via taḍmīn
C) Because there is a grammatical error
D) Because the second object is actually a circumstantial accusative

Key Takeaway
Taḍmīn is a key hermeneutical tool in Hanafi usul to resolve apparent grammatical anomalies by merging verb meanings.

Mubālaghah and Word Order: Progression vs. Completion
Analyzing composition, mubalaghah, and metaphors in explanatory context

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the justification for ordering words with different degrees of hyperbole in classical texts, using the concepts of taraqqī (progression) and tamīm (completion).

Core Idea
Hyperbole does not always require ascending order; context can justify presenting stronger before weaker as completion.

The passage addresses a perceived violation of the rule that words should be ordered from weaker to stronger hyperbole. The commentator provides three responses, ranging from redefining the forms to invoking contextual relevance. This lesson explores how classical scholars justify apparent deviations from rhetorical norms, emphasizing that the author's intention (majmaʿ) can override standard principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rhetorical rule
Recognize the norm that eloquence requires progression from lesser to greater mubālaghah.
This is a standard principle in balāghah.
2
Recognize the apparent violation
Note that 'muʿwānā' is argued to have stronger hyperbole than 'ẓahīr', yet 'ẓahīr' comes after 'muʿwānā' in the text.
3
Examine the first answer
Reinterpret 'ẓahīr' as a ṣifah mushabbahah implying permanence, making it stronger.
This shifts the analysis from mubālaghah to lexicology.
4
Analyze the second answer
Understand that the ordering is not progression (taraqqī) but completion (tamīm): the stronger meaning is stated first, then the remaining nuance.
This is a context-based justification.
5
Consider the third answer
Link 'ẓahīr' to 'ẓahīr al-qawm' to increase its hyperbole.
This derivation from a compound phrase elevates the word's meaning.

This passage is a multi-faceted linguistic and exegetical commentary typical of sharḥ works. Key Terms
صفت مشبہ (ṣifah mushabbahah): A morphological form indicating a permanent quality, functioning like an adjective but typically intransitive.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference, a method of departing from qiyas for a stronger reason.
استصحاب (istiṣḥāb): Continuity of obligation or ruling in the absence of evidence of change.

Grammatical Precision in Textual Analysis: The Case of Hamida as a Hal
Explains grammatical choices in a fiqh text's introduction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the grammatical reasons for the omission of the conjunction and the impossibility of making 'hamid' a hal from 'yaqulu' in the new recension, and apply these analytical methods to evaluate textual variants in usul al-fiqh texts.

Core Idea
Grammatical precision safeguards equality in praise; textual recensions must be reconciled without violating syntax.

This passage delves into the grammatical choices made by the author of al-Tawdih in his introductory praise. The commentator explains why the conjunction (atf) was omitted to preserve equal status between basmalah and hamd, and then demonstrates why 'hamid' cannot be a hal from 'yaqulu' in the new recension due to two specific syntactic flaws. This lesson equips you with tools to analyze such textual nuances, which are critical for precise legal interpretation in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual variants
Recognize that the passage discusses two recensions (nuskha qadima and nuskha jadida) of al-Tawdih, differing by the inclusion of 'wa ba'da fa'inna al-'abda'. Understand which recension is being used for each argument.
The old version lacks the extra phrase, making 'yaqulu' the main verb; the new version includes it.
2
Understand the omission of the conjunction
The author omitted 'wa' (and) before 'hamid' to avoid implying that hamd is subordinate to basmalah. The rule that 'ma'tuf is subordinate to ma'tuf alayh' would suggest basmalah is original and hamd derivative, contradicting the intended equality.
This is a deliberate stylistic choice to preserve equal weight between two acts of praise.
3
Analyze the proposed hal from yaqulu
In the new recension, making 'hamid' a hal from the pronoun of 'yaqulu' is rejected due to two flaws: (1) it places the conjunction waw in the middle of the conjuncted element, and (2) it forces a part of the clause ('hamid') to precede 'inna', violating its required initial position.
These are purely grammatical objections; a third proposed flaw (tautology) is rebutted by reinterpreting the possessive in 'ba'da'.
4
Evaluate the recension preference
The old recension allows the hal from yaqulu without flaws, but that would subordinate hamd to basmalah. The new recension was likely introduced to fix this and achieve equality, even at the cost of making the hal analysis more complex.
The commentator notes that hal from ibtada'a (outside the book) is less ideal than from yaqulu (inside), but the new recension resolves the equality issue.

Source Text

رو ترک العاطف:۔ یہ عبارت کا پانچواں حصہ ہے اس عبارت سے بھی غرض شارح سوال مقدر کا جواب دینا ہے۔ سوال:۔ جب مقصود سمیہ وتحمید میں برابری پیدا کرنا تھا تو یہ برابری عطف کی صورت میں بھی ہو سکتی تھی ۔ یعنی حامد سے پہلے واوعاطفہ ذکر کر کے مبر کا پر عطف ڈال دیں تو بھی ان میں برابری پیدا ہو جاتی ہے۔ مصنف نے ایسا کیوں نہیں کیا ؟ جواب:۔ اہل نحو کے ہاں معطوف ۔ معطوف علیہ کے تابع ہوتا ہے اور معطوف علیہ اصل ہوتا ہے ۔ تو اس سے یہ وہم ہو سکتا ہے کہ شاید مصنف نے بسم اللہ سے اصالنا تبرک حاصل کیا ہے۔ اور حمد سے تبعاً تیرک حاصل کیا ۔ حالانکہ حمد بھی بسم اللہ کی طرح مقصود بالذات ہے تو اس طرح دونوں میں تسو یہ باقی نہ رہتا۔
حامد کو یقول کی ضمیر فاعل سے حال نہ بنانے کی وجوہ
ولا تجوز:۔ یہ عبارت کا چھٹا حصہ ہے اس عبارت سے غرض شارح سوال مقدر کا جواب دینا ہے۔ سوال:۔ آپ نے حامدا کو ابدی کی انا ضمیر سے حال بنایا ہے یقول کی ھو ضمیر سے حال کیوں نہیں بنایا ؟ ( جو یقول لفظ بعد کے بعد موجود ہے)۔
جواب:۔ دراصل متن کے دو نسخے ہیں۔ ایک نسخہ قدیمہ دوسرا نسخہ جدیدہ نسخہ قدیمہ کی عبارت اس طرح ہے ۔ مجلیا ومصلیا يقول العبد المتوسل ۔ یعنی نسخہ قدیمہ میں وبعد فان العبد کا اضافہ نہیں ہے۔ اور نسخہ جدیدہ میں وبعد فان العبدالح کا اضافہ موجود ہے تو اب نسخہ جدیدہ کے مطابق حامد کو یقول کی ضمیر سے حال نہیں بنا سکتے کیونکہ اس صورت میں دو خرابیاں لازم آتی ہیں۔ (۱) قاعدہ اور ضابطہ ہے کہ حرف عطف معطوف سے مقدم ہوتا ہے اثناء معطوف میں نہیں ہوتا۔ اب اگر حامد اکو ضمیر یقول سے حال بنائیں تو واو عاطفہ کا اثناء معطوف میں آنا لازم آئے گا۔ وہ اس طرح کہ لفظ و بعد کے بعد معطوف ہے اور واؤ سے پہلے معطوف علیہ ہے۔ اور حامد اضمیر یقول سے حال ہے اور حال ذو الحال کے لیے قید ہوتا ہے۔ تو معطوف کا ایک حصہ یعنی یقول واؤ کے بعد ہو جائے گا اور ایک حصہ یعنی حامد اپہلے آ جائے گا اور یہ درست نہیں (۲) دوسری خرابی یہ لازم آتی ہے کہ لفظ ان صدارت کلام کو چاہتا ہے اور اگر حامد کو ضمیر یقول سے حال بنائیں تو یقول کا ایک جز یعنی حامد اان پر مقدم ہو جائے گا۔ اور ان کی صدارت ٹوٹ جائے گی جو کہ درست نہیں ۔
فائدہ:۔ بعض حضرات نے فرمایا ہے کہ ایک تیسری خرابی بھی لازم آتی ہے کہ اگر حامدا کو ضمیر یقول سے حال بنا ئیں تو معنی خلاف مقصود ہو جائے گا کیونکہ اس وقت معنی ہوگا۔ بعد الحمد والصلوة يقول خامد ا حمد وصلوٰۃ کے بعد بندہ کہتا ہے اس حال میں کہ حمد بیان کرنے والا ہے اور یہ تکرار محض ہے لیکن یہ درست نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں بعد کا مضاف الیہ حمد وصلوٰۃ نہیں ہوگی ۔ بلکہ ابتداء بالتسمیہ ہوگا۔ تقدیر عبارت ہوگی۔ بعد الابتداء بالتسمیہ یقول حامدا ۔ یعنی ابتداء بالتسمیہ کے بعد کہتا ہے بندہ اس حال میں کہ وہ تعریف کرنے والا ہے۔ تو اس صورت میں معنی درست ہے اور تکرار لازم نہیں آتا ۔ ہاں اگر نسخہ قدیمہ ہو تو اس وقت ظاہر یہی ہے کہ حامدا؟ ضمیر یقول سے حال ہے کیونکہ اس وقت کوئی خرابی لازم نہیں آتی ۔
سوال: نسخہ قدیمہ میں اگر خامد ا کو یقول کی ضمیر سے حال بنائیں تو تسمیہ و حمید میں برابری باقی نہیں رہتی حالانکہ مقصودان دونوں میں تسویہ اور برابری پیدا کرنا ہے تو شاریخ نے یہ کیسے کہ دیا کہ ظاہر یہی ہے کہ یقول کی ضمیر سے حال ہے۔ جواب: - حامد کو اگر ضمیر یقول سے حال بنائیں تو یہ حال ہو گا۔ داخل کتاب سے اور اگر ابتدئی کی ضمیر سے حال بنائیں تو یہ حال ہو گا خارج کتاب سے کیونکہ خود مصنف نے تنقیح میں تصریح کی ہے۔ کہ بسم اللہ کتاب کا جز نہیں ہے ۔ لہذا داخل کتاب سے حال بنانا خارج کتاب سے حال سے بنانے سے زیادہ بہتر ہے۔ سوال:۔ یہ تو کوئی ضروری نہیں کہ مصنف نے تنقیح میں اگر تسمیہ کو کتاب کا جز نہیں بنایا تو توضیح میں بھی تسمیہ کو جزء کتاب نہ بنایا ہو۔ بلکہ تسویہ کے قرینہ سے یہ معلوم ہوتا ہے۔ کہ تسمیہ کتاب کا جزء ہے پھر جب یہ کتاب کا جزء ہے تو ابتدئی سے حال بنانے کی صورت میں بھی داخل کتاب سے حال ہوگا۔
جواب: اگر مان بھی لیں کہ تسمیہ کتاب کا جزء ہے پھر بھی کہتے ہیں کہ حامد ا کو یقول کی ضمیر سے حال بنانا ظاہر ہے کیونکہ بسم اللہ کے مابعد کوبسم اللہ سے متعلق کرنا بین المصنفین معہو دو متعارف نہیں ہے۔
فائدہ:۔ شاید اسی وجہ سے نسخہ قدیمہ تبدیل کیا گیا ہے تاکہ یقول کی ضمیر سے حال نہ بنے اور تسویسی تحقیق ہو جائے۔
May they curse and abuse me, but not the name of God. Let me be ruined, but not God. Summary: The reason for deviating from presenting the basmalah in the verse is clear, and consensus on presenting basmalah is when there is no preventing need. 
And regarding the omission of the conjunction: This is the fifth part of the phrase; by this phrase the commentator intends to answer an implicit question. Question: Since the objective was to create equality between basmalah and tahmid, this equality could also have been achieved by using a conjunction, i.e., by placing a waw before hamid and attaching it to the preceding word, then equality would be achieved. Why did the author not do that? Answer: According to grammarians, the conjuncted (معطوف) is subordinate to the conjuncted-to (معطوف علیہ), and the conjuncted-to is the original. So this might create the impression that the author obtained blessing from basmalah as an original, and from hamd as a derivative, whereas hamd is also intended in itself like basmalah, so equality would not remain. 
Reasons for not making hamid a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu: 
Wa la yajuz: This is the sixth part of the phrase; by this phrase the commentator intends to answer an implicit question. Question: You made hamid a hal from the pronoun of abtada'a (I begin), why did you not make it a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu (he says) which comes after? Answer: Actually, there are two versions of the text: an old version and a new version. The old version reads: 'mujalliyan wa musalliyan yaqulu al-'abdu al-mutawassil', meaning the old version does not include 'wa ba'da fa'inna al-'abda'. The new version includes 'wa ba'da fa'inna al-'abda' etc. Now according to the new version, one cannot make hamid a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu because that would entail two flaws: (1) The rule is that the conjunction letter precedes the conjuncted, it does not occur in the middle of the conjuncted. If we make hamid a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu, then the waw would necessarily appear in the middle of the conjuncted, like this: 'wa ba'da' is conjuncted, the waw is before it, the conjuncted-to is before the waw. And hamid is a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu, and a hal is a condition for the owner of the state. So part of the conjuncted, i.e., yaqulu, would come after the waw, and part, i.e., hamid, would come before it, which is incorrect. (2) The second flaw is that the word 'inna' requires being at the beginning of the speech, and if we make hamid a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu, then a part of yaqulu, i.e., hamid, would precede 'inna', breaking its precedence, which is incorrect. 
Benefit: Some scholars said that a third flaw also arises: if we make hamid a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu, the meaning would be contrary to the intended, because then the meaning would be: 'after hamd and salat, the slave says, while being a praiser (hamid),' and this is pure repetition. But this is not correct because in that case the possessive adjective of 'ba'da' would not be hamd and salat, but rather al-ibtida' bi al-tasmiyah. The implied phrase would be: 'ba'da al-ibtida'i bi al-tasmiyah yaqulu hamidan', i.e., after beginning with basmalah, the slave says while being a praiser. So the meaning is correct and no repetition is entailed. Yes, if it is the old version, then it is apparent that hamid is a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu, because then no flaw arises. 
Question: In the old version, if we make hamid a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu, then equality between tasmiyah and tahmid is not preserved, although the intention is to create equality between the two. So how did the commentator say that it is apparent that it is a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu? Answer: If we make hamid a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu, it becomes a hal from within the book, and if we make it a hal from the pronoun of ibtada'a, it becomes a hal from outside the book, because the author himself explicitly stated in al-Tanqih that basmalah is not part of the book. Therefore, making a hal from within the book is better than making one from outside the book. Question: It is not necessary that because the author did not make tasmiyah part of the book in al-Tanqih, he also did not make it part of the book in al-Tawdih. Rather, the context of equality indicates that tasmiyah is part of the book; then when it is part of the book, making it a hal from ibtada'a also makes it from within the book. Answer: Even if we accept that tasmiyah is part of the book, still we say that making hamid a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu is more apparent because relating what comes after basmalah to basmalah is not customary among authors. 
Benefit: Perhaps for this reason the old version was changed so that it is not made a hal from the pronoun of yaqulu and equality is achieved.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical analysis of the opening of a fiqh text, specifically why the author chose to make 'hamid' (a praiser) a hal from the pronoun of 'ibtada'a' (I begin) rather than from 'yaqulu' (he says). The commentator explains the reasons by considering two versions of the text (old and new). The old version lacked 'wa ba'da fa'inna al-'abda', making 'yaqulu' the main verb. In the new version, with the addition, making 'hamid' a hal from 'yaqulu' causes two flaws: it would place the conjunction waw in the middle of the conjuncted, and it would break the required initial position of 'inna'. A third flaw (tautology) is dismissed by reinterpretation. The discussion also addresses concerns about equality between basmalah and hamd, and whether tasmiyah is part of the book. The commentator concludes that the old version's change was intended to achieve proper equality.

Key Terms
حال (hal): A grammatical state indicating the manner of the action, typically a verbal noun or adjective describing the subject or object at the time of the action.
عطف (atf): Grammatical conjunction, usually with waw, linking elements of equal status.
نسخہ قدیمہ (nuskha qadīma): The older recension of the text (of al-Tawdih) without the phrase 'wa ba'da fa'inna al-'abda'.
نسخہ جدیدہ (nuskha jadīda): The newer recension of the text that includes the additional phrase 'wa ba'da fa'inna al-'abda'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is making 'hamid' a hal from the pronoun of 'yaqulu' not allowed in the new version?
A) It creates a tautology in meaning.
B) It would require the conjunction waw to appear in the middle of the conjuncted, violating grammatical rules.
C) It makes tasmiyah part of the book, which contradicts al-Tanqih.
D) It breaks the required initial position of 'inna' and is therefore impermissible.

Key Takeaway
Grammatical analysis is essential in usul al-fiqh textual criticism; a single conjunction or recension variant can determine the meaning and legal implication of a text.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Thana' as Kinayah: Avoiding Repetition in Praise
Defends thana' against repetition charge by defining it as kinayah for intention of glorification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why thana' is not a repetition of hamd and classify kinayah within haqiqah according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Thana' is kinayah for intention of glorification, making it broader than hamd and avoiding repetition.

In this passage, the commentator defends the phrase 'thana' to Him' against the charge of redundancy after 'hamd'. The key lies in understanding thana' as kinayah—a term used in its original meaning but intending a necessary concomitant. This classification, rooted in Hanafi usul, shows that thana' encompasses both verbal and non-verbal praise, thus no repetition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The commentator presents an objection: if both hamd and thana' are verbal, mentioning thana' after hamd causes repetition.
The objection assumes thana' is purely verbal like hamd.
2
Analyze the response
The commentator defines thana' as the intention of glorification (qasd ta'zim), including words and deeds, making the second praise more general.
This is the core of the defense.
3
Understand kinayah
The response uses kinayah: the term 'inān (rein) is used to mean turning attention, which is a necessary concomitant. The original meaning is retained, but the intended meaning is different.
Kinayah is not majaz; it stays within haqiqah.
4
Classify kinayah within haqiqah
Citing Bahā' al-Dīn al-Sakti, the text establishes that kinayah is a subset of haqiqah because the word is used in its original meaning, albeit not as the primary intent.
This is the Hanafi position.
5
Apply to the dispute
Conclude that thana' as kinayah is not metaphorical; it remains haqiqah, thus avoiding repetition and providing a stronger argument.
The practical effect: thana' includes non-verbal praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولہ الثالث:۔ وجوہ ثلاثہ میں سے تیسری وجہ کا بیان ہے کہ جس کا حاصل یہ ہے اللہ تعالٰی دنیا میں بھی حمد کے مستحق ہیں اس لیے کہ ایک تو دلائل قدرت سے اللہ تعالیٰ کی ذات کا کمال ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ دوسرا بندوں کی طرف اللہ تعالیٰ کے جو انعامات نازل ہوتے ہیں اس وجہ سے بھی وہ مستحق حمد ہیں۔ اور آخرت میں مستحق محمد ہیں۔ بوجہ اللہ تعالیٰ کی قدرت اور ایسی نعمتوں کا مشاہدہ کرنے کے کہ جرمی کو نہ آنکھوں نے دیکھا ہے نہ کانوں نے سنا اور نہ دل پر کھنکیں جیسا کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے ارشاد سے معلوم ہوا جون وَلَهُ الْحَمْدُ فِي الأولى وَالْآخِرَةِ ، دوسری جگہ ارشاد فرمایا وَ آخِرُ دَعْوَاهُمْ أَنِ الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ رَبِّ الْعَلَمِينَ ، تو مصن انے اولا کہہ کر استحقاق دنیا وی کی طرف اشارہ کر دیا۔ اور ثانیاً کہہ کر استحقاق اخروی کی طرف اشارہ کر دیا۔ یعنی میں اللہ تعالی کی تعریف کرتا ہوں دنیا میں اور آخرت میں۔
حمد کے بعد ثناللہ میں تکرار نہیں ہے
قولہ فان قلت :۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا آٹھواں حصہ ہے۔ اس عبارت سے غرض شاریخ تیسری صورت پر اعتراض کرنا . ہے۔ جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ حمد بھی زبان کے ساتھ خاص ہے۔ اور ثناء بھی زبان کے ساتھ خاص ہے۔ اور اولا سے مراد دنیا میں تعریف ہے اور ثانیا سے مراد آخرت میں تعریف ہے۔ تو جب دنیاو آخرت میں زبان سے تعریف ہو چکی تو والعنان الثناء الیہ ثانیا میں تکرار حض ہے کیونکہ اس میں بھی ثناء کا ذکر ہے اور یہ بھی زبان سے ہوتی ہے۔
قلت: یہ شرح کی عبارت کا حصہ نہم ہے۔ غرض شاریخ اس مذکورہ سوال کا جواب دینا ہے۔ جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ ثناء سے مراد قصد تعظیم ہے یعنی ہر وہ چیز جس سے اللہ تعالیٰ کا تقرب حاصل کیا جائے چاہے وہ اقوال کے قبیل سے ہو یا افعال کے قبیل سے ہو تو اس کا قصد کرنا ثناء ہے تو گویا ثناء سے مراد شکر ہے۔ تو پہلے جملہ میں اللہ تعالیٰ کی تعریف زبان سے ہوئی اور دوسرے جملے میں اللہ تعالی کی تعریف زبان ، دل اور اعضاء سب سے ہوئی تو یہ قیم بعد از تخصیص ہے۔ لہذا تکرار لازم نہیں آتا ۔ سوال :۔ ثناء سے تعظیم والا معنی تب مراد لیا جا سکتا ہے کہ ثناء اقوال و افعال سب کو عام ہو ۔ حالانکہ یہ تو زبان کے ساتھ خاص ہے۔ اور اگر یہ مجاز ہے تو پھر مجاز احمد سے بھی عام معنی شکر والا مراد لیا جا سکتا ہے۔ تو پھر بھی ولعنان الثناءالیہ ثانیا کوذکر کرنے میں تکرار ہوگا۔
جواب : یہ کنایہ ہے قصد تعظیم سے کیونکہ عرفا کسی کی طرف لگام پھیرنا یہ لازم ہے اس کی طرف متوجہ ہونے کو تو گویا لازم بول کر ملزوم مراد لیا ہے ۔ اور یہی کنایہ ہے۔ خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ ثناء کا مجازی معنی مراد نہیں ہے بلکہ کنا یہ استعمال کیا ہے اور کنایہ محققین کے نزدیک حقیقت ہی میں داخل ہے چنانچہ علامہ بہاؤ الدین سکتی عروس الافراح میں فرماتے ہیں کہ لفظ دو حال سے بہاؤالد خالی نہیں۔ معنی موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہوگا یا غیر موضوع لہ میں۔ اگر معنی غیر موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہو تو مجاز ہے اور اگر معنی موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہو تو دو حال سے خالی نہیں کہ معنی موضوع لہ مقصود بالذات ہوگا یا مقصود بالتبع ہوگا اور مقصود بالذات کو لازم ہوگا ۔ اول حقیقت ہے اور ثانی کنایہ ہے تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ کنایہ بھی معنی موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہوتا ہے۔ اور یہ بھی حقیقت کا ایک فرد ہے۔
His statement: The third: Explanation of the third of the three aspects. Its summary is that Allah is deserving of praise in this world because, first, the perfection of Allah's essence is proven by proofs of power. Second, because of the blessings that Allah bestows upon servants, He is deserving of praise. And in the Hereafter, He is deserving of praise due to His power and the witnessing of such blessings that no eye has seen, no ear has heard, and no heart has conceived, as is known from His statement: 'And to Him belongs all praise in the first and the last.' In another place He said: 'And the last of their call is: Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds.' So the author indicated worldly deserving with 'first' and otherworldly deserving with 'secondly', i.e., I praise Allah in this world and the Hereafter. There is no repetition after praise in 'thana' to Allah'.
His statement: 'For if you say': This is the eighth part of the commentary's text. By this expression, the commentator intends to object to the third form. Its summary is that praise is specific to the tongue, and thana' is also specific to the tongue. 'First' means praise in this world, and 'secondly' means praise in the Hereafter. So when praise by the tongue has occurred in this world and the Hereafter, then in 'and the reins of thana' to Him secondly' there is repetition, because thana' is also mentioned there and it is also done by the tongue.
'I say': This is the ninth part of the commentary's text. The commentator intends to answer this objection. Its summary is that thana' means the intention of glorification, i.e., everything by which one seeks nearness to Allah, whether it is of the category of words or deeds. So intending that is thana'. Thus thana' means gratitude. So in the first sentence, praise of Allah was by the tongue, and in the second sentence, praise of Allah was by the tongue, heart, and all limbs. So this is a general after specification. Therefore repetition does not necessarily arise.
Question: The meaning of glorification from thana' can only be taken if thana' is general to both words and deeds. Whereas it is specific to the tongue. And if it is majaz, then from the majaz of praise, a general meaning of gratitude can also be taken. Then there would still be repetition in mentioning 'and the reins of thana' to Him secondly'.
Answer: This is kinayah for the intention of glorification, because in custom, turning the reins towards someone is necessary for turning towards him. So the necessary is mentioned and the necessitated is intended. This is kinayah. The summary is that thana' is not used in a metaphorical meaning but as kinayah. And kinayah is included in haqiqah according to the verifiers, as Allamah Baha'uddin al-Sakti says in 'Arus al-Afrah': A word is free from two states: it is used either in its original meaning or not. If used in other than its original meaning, it is majaz. If used in its original meaning, it does not lack two states: the original meaning is either intended essentially or intended subsidiarily and is necessary for the essentially intended. The first is haqiqah, the second is kinayah. So it is known that kinayah is also used in the original meaning and is a type of haqiqah.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the third aspect of the praise of Allah. The commentator first explains the third wajh (aspect) from the original matn: Allah is worthy of praise in this world due to His perfect power and favors, and in the Hereafter due to witnessing unimaginable blessings. The author uses 'firstly' and 'secondly' to refer to worldly and otherworldly deserving. Then an objection is raised: if hamd and thana' are both specific to the tongue, mentioning thana' after hamd causes repetition because both refer to verbal praise. The commentator responds by defining thana' as the intention of glorification (qasd al-ta'zim), which includes both words and deeds. Thus the first sentence praises by tongue, the second by tongue, heart, and limbs — a general after specification, so no repetition. A further objection suggests thana' cannot mean intention of glorification unless it is general, but it is linguistically specific to the tongue. If taken as majaz, then hamd could also be taken in a general sense, leading back to repetition. The answer refutes this by classifying 'thana' as kinayah (metonymy), not majaz. Kinayah uses a word in its original meaning but intends a necessary concomitant. Here, 'turning the reins' (the term 'inān) is used, which literally means rein but as kinayah indicates turning attention. The commentator then cites a definition from Bahā' al-Dīn al-Sakti: haqiqah is when the original meaning is essentially intended; kinayah is when the original meaning is intended but as a necessary concomitant, and it is a subset of haqiqah. Thus thana' as kinayah is not metaphorical but remains within haqiqah, avoiding repetition.

Key Terms
ثناء (thana'): Intention of glorification (qasd ta'zim) that includes both words and deeds.
كنایة (kinayah): Using a word in its original meaning but intending a necessary concomitant, considered a type of haqiqah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is thana' not a repetition of hamd?
A) Because thana' is a metaphor for gratitude
B) Because thana' is a kinayah for the intention of glorification, which includes both words and deeds
C) Because hamd is for this world and thana' for the Hereafter
D) Because thana' is specific to deeds

Key Takeaway
Thana' is kinayah for intention of glorification, encompassing words and deeds, and kinayah is a subset of haqiqah in Hanafi usul.

Exclusivity by Word Order and Inclusive Lam
Second response to repetition objection: distinguishing locus vs object, hasr, and inclusive lam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply Hanafi usul tools of hasr (exclusivity via word order) and alif lam istighraqi to determine when a textual repetition adds a new legal benefit, thereby avoiding unnecessary repetition.

Core Idea
Word order can create exclusive attribution; an inclusive definite article can expand scope—both generate new meanings, defeating claims of pointless repetition.

In this passage, the commentator of the basmala (in a work of usul al-fiqh) responds to a second objection. The objection is that the two expressions 'Hamid Allah' and 'and the reins of thana' to Him secondly' seem redundant: both praise Allah. The commentator provides a second answer, analyzing the two phrases through the lenses of place of praise (mahall) and object of praise (muta'allaq). He shows that the first phrase specifies the praise as coming from the tongue, while the second, through word order inversion and the definite article 'al-', establishes that ALL praises are exclusively for Allah. This lesson trains you to apply these linguistic-legal tools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection is that two praise expressions in the basmala are repetitive. You need to locate the question in the text: it asks how the second phrase differs from the first.
The objection is introduced after 'Wa lau qila' or similar; here it is 'Sawal: ye ikhtisas to Hamid Allah mein bhi...'
2
Distinguish locus and object
The commentator says praise has two aspects: mahall (locus, i.e., the praising tongue) and muta'allaq (object, i.e., the One praised). Recognize that 'Hamid Allah' focuses on the locus (the act of praising from the speaker), while the second phrase focuses on the object.
In usul al-fiqh, this distinction is crucial for deriving different rulings.
3
Analyze hasr in the second phrase
The phrase 'and the reins of thana' to Him secondly' (وَالْعِنَانَ الثَّناءِ إِلَيْهِ ثَانِيًا) advances the prepositional phrase 'to Him' before 'secondly'. This word order inversion creates hasr (exclusivity): the reins of praise are turned only to Allah, not to any creation.
Hanafi usul recognizes that advancing the ma'mul (object) over the 'amil (operator) can indicate hasr.
4
Analyze the inclusive lam on 'al-thana'
The word 'al-thana' (الثناء) has the definite article with inclusive meaning (alif lam istighraqi). This indicates all praises collectively, unlike the first phrase where only one individual praise is specified.
Definite articles can be for 'ahd (specific) or istighraq (generic inclusive); here it's the latter.
5
Conclude new benefit
Since the first phrase specifies a single praise from a single praiser, and the second specifies all praises collectively and exclusively for Allah, they convey different meanings. Therefore, no repetition occurs.
This method of resolving apparent repetition by finding distinct linguistic features is a core Hanafi usul skill.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولہ وفیہ اشارہ :۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا دسواں حصہ ہے اس عبارت میں شاریخ اسی سوال کے جواب ثانی کی طرف اشارہ کیا ہے۔ جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ حمد کی دو حیثیتیں ہیں۔ (۱) محل (۲) متعلق ۔ تو حامد اللہ میں محل حمد میں تخصیص ثابت ہوئی یعنی زبان سے تعریف کی اور والعنان الثناء الیہ ثانیا میں متعلق کے اعتبار سے اختصاص ثابت ہوا کہ تعریف صرف اللہ کے ساتھ خاص ہے کیونکہ اس دوسرے جملہ میں عامل کو معمول پر مقدم کرنے سے یعنی الی کو ثانیا پر مقدم کرنے کے اعتبار سے حصر ثابت ہوا ہے یعنی ہر وہ آدمی جو علوم اسلامیہ کو شروع کرنے والا ہو اس کے لیے ضروری ہے کہ مخلوق کی طرف سے نظریں ہٹا کر تعریف کی لگاموں کو صرف اللہ کی طرف پھیر لے کیونکہ شاہ کی مستحق صرف وہی ذات ہے اور کوئی ذات نہیں اگر یہ دوسرا جملہ ذکر نہ کرتے تو جمد کے اختصاص باللہ کا فائدہ حاصل نہ ہوتا اور تکرار تب لازم آتا ہے جب کلام نئے فائدہ پر مشتمل نہ ہو۔ سوال:۔ یہ اختصاص تو حامد اللہ میں بھی پایا جاتا ہے کیونکہ للہ میں لام اختصاص کی ہے تو متعلق کے اعتبار سےحمد اللہ تعالیٰ کے ساتھ خاص ہوئی ۔ جواب :۔ وہاں حمد کا صرف ایک فرد جو حامد سے سرزد ہو رہا ہے خاص ہے اللہ تعالیٰ کے لیے اور یہاں الثناء پر الف لام استغراقی ہونے کی وجہ سے جمیع محامد کا اختصاص ہے اللہ تعالیٰ کے ساتھ تو گویا پہلے جملے میں تقلیل حمد ہے اور اس جملہ میں کثیر حمد تو ہے تو پھر بھی تکرار لازم نہیں آتا۔ فلا اشکال۔
His statement: 'And in it is an indication': This is the tenth part of the commentary's text. In this expression, the commentator indicates the second answer to the same question. Its summary is that praise has two aspects: (1) locus (mahall), (2) object of reference (muta'allaq). In 'Hamid Allah', specification in the locus of praise is established, i.e., praise by the tongue. And in 'and the reins of thana' to Him secondly', specification with respect to the object is established, that praise is specific only to Allah, because in this second sentence, by advancing the agent over the object, i.e., advancing 'to' over 'secondly', exclusivity (hasr) is established, meaning: every person who is a beginner of Islamic sciences must turn away glances from creation and turn the reins of praise only towards Allah, because the King is deserving only of that Essence and no other. If this second sentence were not mentioned, the benefit of the specification of praise to Allah would not be obtained. Repetition only occurs when speech does not contain a new benefit. Question: This specification is also found in 'Hamid Allah', because 'li' in 'lillah' is for specification, so with respect to the object, praise is specific to Allah. Answer: There, only one individual praise that occurs from the praiser is specific to Allah. Here, due to the inclusive lam (alif lam istighraqi) on 'al-thana'', all praises collectively are specified for Allah. So in the first sentence there is a few praises (taqlil), and in this sentence there is many praises (kathir). Therefore, no repetition is necessary. So no problem.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage provides a second response to the objection of repetition. The commentator explains that praise (hamd) has two aspects: the locus (mahall) where praise originates (the tongue) and the object (muta'allaq) to whom praise is directed. In the first phrase 'Hamid Allah', the specification is on the locus: it is praise from the tongue. In the second phrase 'and the reins of thana' to Him secondly', the specification is on the object: that all praise is exclusively for Allah. This exclusivity is highlighted by the word order 'to Him secondly', which implies that the reins of praise are turned only to Allah, not to any creation. If the second sentence were omitted, the benefit of exclusive devotion of all praise to Allah would be lost. A further objection states that even in 'Hamid Allah', the lam (li) indicates specification for Allah, so the object is already specified. The commentator responds: in the first sentence, only the specific act of praise from the praiser is specified for Allah (i.e., this one praise is for Allah). In the second sentence, due to the inclusive definite article 'al-' on 'al-thana', it refers to all praises collectively, indicating that every possible praise belongs exclusively to Allah. Thus the first expresses a limited number of praises (taqlil), while the second expresses abundance (kathir). Therefore, they convey different benefits—intensity versus manner—and no repetition occurs.

Key Terms
حصر (hasr): Exclusivity, achieved by advancing the agent over the object in Arabic sentence structure.
الف لام استغراقی (alif lam istighraqi): Definite article that indicates inclusion of all instances of the noun.
محل (mahall): Locus; here, the place where praise originates (the tongue).
متعلق (muta'allaq): Object of reference; here, the One to whom praise is directed (Allah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key linguistic feature in the second phrase that distinguishes it from the first and provides a new legal benefit?
A) The use of the word 'thana' instead of 'hamd'
B) The advancement of the prepositional phrase 'to Him' before 'secondly', creating exclusivity, and the inclusive definite article on 'al-thana'
C) The repetition of the word 'Allah'
D) The mention of 'reins' (inān) indicating control

Key Takeaway
A textual repetition is not redundant if linguistic features (word order, definite article type) generate a distinct and additional legal benefit.

Kinayah as a Subset of Haqiqah
Definitional clarification of kinayah as literal usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish kinayah from haqiqah and majaz, and explain why Hanafi usul classifies kinayah as a type of haqiqah.

Core Idea
Kinayah is literal usage with secondary intention — not metaphor.

This passage from the Sharh al-Talwih clarifies a foundational definition in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the classification of kinayah (allusion). While often mistakenly grouped with majaz (metaphor), the Hanafi school places kinayah under haqiqah (literal usage). Understanding this affects how legal texts are interpreted — especially when a word implies a necessary concomitant rather than the direct meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary categories of word usage
Recognize that a word can be used in its lexical meaning (haqiqah) or in a non-lexical meaning (majaz). The passage begins by establishing this binary.
2
Split haqiqah into direct and secondary intention
Within haqiqah, the literal meaning can be intended directly (primary) or indirectly as a necessary concomitant (secondary). The first is haqiqah proper; the second is kinayah.
The key distinction is the mode of intention (qasd).
3
Apply the definition to a legal example
Consider the phrase 'He entered the house' to imply marriage. In Hanafi usul, this is kinayah because the literal meaning (entering) is used, but the primary intention is marriage (a necessary concomitant). Thus it remains haqiqah, not majaz.
This classification affects rulings in contracts and oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محققین کے نزدیک حقیقت ہی میں داخل ہے چنانچہ علامہ بہاؤ الدین سکتی عروس الافراح میں فرماتے ہیں کہ لفظ دو حال سے بہاؤالد خالی نہیں۔ معنی موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہوگا یا غیر موضوع لہ میں۔ اگر معنی غیر موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہو تو مجاز ہے اور اگر معنی موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہو تو دو حال سے خالی نہیں کہ معنی موضوع لہ مقصود بالذات ہوگا یا مقصود بالتبع ہوگا اور مقصود بالذات کو لازم ہوگا ۔ اول حقیقت ہے اور ثانی کنایہ ہے تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ کنایہ بھی معنی موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہوتا ہے۔ اور یہ بھی حقیقت کا ایک فرد ہے۔
According to the verifiers, it enters into reality. Indeed, Allamah Bahauddin al-Sikti in 'Arus al-Afrah' says that a word is not free from two states: it is used in its lexical meaning or in a non-lexical meaning. If used in a non-lexical meaning, it is metaphorical; if used in its lexical meaning, it is not free from two states: the lexical meaning may be intended by itself, or it may be intended secondarily and necessary for that which is primarily intended. The first is reality (haqiqah), and the second is allusion (kinayah). So it is known that kinayah is also used in the lexical meaning, and it is a subset of reality.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification. The commentator establishes that kinayah (allusion) is a type of haqiqah (literal usage), not majaz (metaphor). The reasoning: a word used in its original meaning is either intended primarily (haqiqah) or secondarily with a necessary connection (kinayah). Thus kinayah shares the same lexical basis as haqiqah. This is a Hanafi usul position distinguishing between direct and indirect literal usage.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): استعمال اللفظ في المعنى الموضوع له بقصد الذات
كنایة (kinayah): استعمال اللفظ في المعنى الموضوع له بقصد التبعية واللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes kinayah from haqiqah?
A) Kinayah uses the literal meaning for a secondary intention, while haqiqah uses it for the primary intention.
B) Kinayah is metaphorical, while haqiqah is literal.
C) Kinayah is only used in poetry.
D) There is no distinction; they are identical.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah is a subset of haqiqah because it uses the literal meaning, but the primary intention is a necessary concomitant.
